  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f8251387-4854-41ca-a5a6-494968953a6f'
      });

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10816162313362290950">Following All the Pretty Horses in Cormac McCarthy's Border Trilogy is a novel whose force of language is matched only by its breadth of experience and depth of thought. In the bootheel of New Mexico hard on the frontier, Billy and Boyd Parham are just boys in the years before the Second World War, but on the cusp of unimaginable events. First comes a trespassing Indian an</span>
  <span id="freeText10816162313362290950" style="display:none">Following All the Pretty Horses in Cormac McCarthy's Border Trilogy is a novel whose force of language is matched only by its breadth of experience and depth of thought. In the bootheel of New Mexico hard on the frontier, Billy and Boyd Parham are just boys in the years before the Second World War, but on the cusp of unimaginable events. First comes a trespassing Indian and the dream of wolves running wild amongst the cattle lately brought onto the plain by settlers - this when all the wisdom of trappers has disappeared along with the trappers themselves. So Billy sets forth at the age of sixteen on an unwitting journey into the souls of boys, animals and men.<br /><br />Having trapped a she-wolf he would restore to the mountains of Mexico, he is long gone and returns to find everything he left behind transformed utterly in his absence. Except his kid brother, Boyd, with whom he strikes out yet again to reclaim what is theirs - thus crossing into "that antique gaze from whence there could be no way back forever."<br /><br />What they find instead, is an extraordinary panoply of fiestas and circuses, dogs, horses and hawks, pilgrims and revolutionaries, grand haciendas and forlorn cantinas, bandits, gypsies and roving tribes, a young girl alone on the road, a mystery in the mountain wilds, and a myth in the making.<br /><br />And in this wider world they fight a war as rageful as the one neither, in the end, will join up for back home. One brother finds his destiny, while the other arrives only at his fate.<br /><br />An essential novel by any measure, and the transfixing middle passage of Cormac McCarthy's ongoing trilogy, The Crossing is luminous and appalling, a book that touches, stops,and starts the heart and mind at once.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10816162313362290950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/674260-is-it-important-to-read-the-border-trilogy">Is it important to read the Border Trilogy in order? Or are the novels stand-alone?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_674260'><a id="like_count_community_question_674260" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/674260?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F365990.The_Crossing">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>

<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/18812322-dan-west"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1364750995i/18812322._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/18812322-dan-west">Dan West</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">They are stand alone novels, but I would tend to recommend reading them in order if you plan to read all three for overarching structural/tonal/themat<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">They are stand alone novels, but I would tend to recommend reading them in order if you plan to read all three for overarching structural/tonal/thematic reasons. All the Pretty Horses and The Crossing don't have much narrative relation to each other, but Cities of the Plain should definitely go third as it is informed by and brings together main characters from the first two novels.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_156502931" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156502931" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156502931?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156502931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17935677039196909376">Enormously affecting. A boy and his father set out to trap a wolf that is preying on their cattle. The man who had trapped them in the past, who opened the plains for countless thousands of cattle to graze is now dead, and the wolves have begun to return to their old hunting grounds from their retreat in Mexico. The father and son try to take up the trapping in the manner of the past master. <i>The Crossing</i> is about many things: the three journeys over four years into Mexico taken by the young Bill</span>
  <span id="freeText17935677039196909376" style="display:none">Enormously affecting. A boy and his father set out to trap a wolf that is preying on their cattle. The man who had trapped them in the past, who opened the plains for countless thousands of cattle to graze is now dead, and the wolves have begun to return to their old hunting grounds from their retreat in Mexico. The father and son try to take up the trapping in the manner of the past master. <i>The Crossing</i> is about many things: the three journeys over four years into Mexico taken by the young Billy Parham; his own crossing into manhood; the crossing of the dead into ever-lasting life, etc. The series of tests Parham sets himself suggest any number of Old World quest narratives. <br /><br />He captures the wolf, a pregnant female, whom he clearly comes to love, and decides to take her back to her native mountains in Mexico. It doesn't work out. When he returns to Cloverdale NM he learns his mother and father have been slaughtered by Indians. He collects his brother, Boyd, from a foster home and they set off for Mexico, ostensibly in search of the seven horses that constitute the family patrimony, though this mission is never so baldly stated. Billy and Boyd no longer have a home in the world and it's as if they are simply adrift in the landscape. They have entered a wild land still torn by endless revolution, where there is no law save the law that comes self made from the actions of peasants, banditos, philosophizing gypsies, itinerant carneys, mothering women, and children. <br /><br />The landscape is beautifully rendered and as active an agent in the narrative as any of the characters Billy and Boyd meet with. I'm leaving a big chunk of the action undescribed, most of it in fact, not because I believe in spoilers (I don't), but because I think that no nimbleness of paraphrase on my part could ever capture the emotional richness, vivid imagery, and sheer narrative power of this fine novel.<br /><br />I like the way emotional tensions are never directly addressed. Much is left unsaid. It's very stoic, Hemingwayesque. Nietzsche said that one repays a mentor badly when one remains a pupil. Hemingway and Faulkner, it's no surprise, were two of McCarthy's models. (There's probably a touch of Louis L'Amour in the mix too.) Yet I believe he has surpassed them in terms of consistency. Both WF and EH were innovators whose late work became mannered, so taken were they of their own voices and styles. McCarthy may be less of a technical virtuoso, but he is no less the stylist, and he is by far the more consistent writer than either of his models. That said, I am almost positive that one of McCarthy’s models here was Faulkner’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128770.The_Unvanquished" title="The Unvanquished by William Faulkner" rel="nofollow noopener">The Unvanquished</a>. The running down of thieves by teenage boys in both novels seems too strikingly similar. <br /><br />Of the seven or so McCarthy novels I've read, <i>The Crossing</i>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/394535.Blood_Meridian__or_the_Evening_Redness_in_the_West" title="Blood Meridian, or the Evening Redness in the West by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood Meridian</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/469571.All_the_Pretty_Horses__The_Border_Trilogy___1_" title="All the Pretty Horses (The Border Trilogy, #1) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">All The Pretty Horses</a> are my favorites. I must read more of him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17935677039196909376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

The Billy character is wonderfully drawn and in particular the first part of the book with the wolf was outstanding.   <span id="freeText15065924848245148009" style="display:none">Far more melancholic than its predecessor All the Pretty Horses, The Crossing is a beautiful if bleak western full of poetry and philosophical musings. The Billy character is wonderfully drawn and in particular the first part of the book with the wolf was outstanding. McCarthy's sparse Hemmingway-esque style lends an austere and yet often humorous tone to the dialogues - particularly those both spoken and unspoken between Billy and Boyd. I appreciate the author's reluctance to dummy down the story and challenge the reader constantly throughout. Looking forward to completing the trilogy now with Cities of the Plain.</span>

<div id="review_2077192247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2077192247" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2077192247?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=xx2018-completed">xx2018-completed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2077192247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2891546413070196469"><b>The Border Trilogy – Part 2 of 3</b><br/><br/>It is in the early 1940’s when Billy Pelham sets out on his first of three trips from his home to Mexico. He is sixteen and his first trip is to reunite a female wolf about to pup with others of her kind in the mountains where she came from.<br/><br/>When he returns home to New Mexico from his journey, his life as he knew it has changed. The family’s horses have been stolen and he and his 14 year old brother Boyd set out to retrieve them. During this longer journey, Billy t</span>
  <span id="freeText2891546413070196469" style="display:none"><b>The Border Trilogy – Part 2 of 3</b><br /><br />It is in the early 1940’s when Billy Pelham sets out on his first of three trips from his home to Mexico. He is sixteen and his first trip is to reunite a female wolf about to pup with others of her kind in the mountains where she came from.<br /><br />When he returns home to New Mexico from his journey, his life as he knew it has changed. The family’s horses have been stolen and he and his 14 year old brother Boyd set out to retrieve them. During this longer journey, Billy turns 17 and his brother turns 15 – and he falls in love and disappears with his young love. Billy makes his way back home again and spends time working on various ranches and other jobs to save up enough to bring his brother home, along with his girl if need be.<br /><br />Billy also tries several times to join the army once war is declared (WWII) but he can’t pass the medical examination because of a heart murmur. Eventually he accumulates an extra horse to use as a pack animal and has outfitted himself with everything he needs to find his brother. By this time, Billy is 20 years old and has more life experience than he had on his first two journeys south.<br /><br />Along the way of his third journey, he meets with several other travelers as well as people who give him food and shelter when needed. The people he meets all have stories to tell and their own versions of life they want to share: <i>
  <b>He said that most men were in their lives like the carpenter whose work went so slowly for the dullness of his tools that he had not time to sharpen them.</b>
</i><br /><br />There are some rough scenes in this book: man’s cruelty to man, man’s cruelty to animals, and man’s cruelty to himself. Those are themes that run through this novel, but in the people’s stories and in their actions there are also many acts of kindness, of compassion, and of caring.<br /><br />Once again, Cormac McCarthy has written a novel that feels real and gritty and harsh. His ability to relate the trials and difficulties of the journeys Billy makes while also holding and lightly weaving in a thread of hope and optimism is nothing short of magical.<br /><br />Initially I did not intend to read this Border Trilogy back-to-back, yet long before I had finished the first book I knew that I had to continue through all three. The writing is exceptional, the characters authentic and sympathetic, while the adventures are compulsively readable. I am very much looking forward to the concluding “chapter” of this amazing Trilogy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2891546413070196469" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1114796489" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1114796489" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1114796489?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1114796489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15383686736334337006">One decision, as innocent as it may be, can fuck up your life forever. Now, you can live in fear and hide yourself away, or you can keep making those decisions and hope for the best, and if and when the shit hits the fan, you can stand strong and push on.<br/><br/>That's life. That's <i>The Crossing</i>.<br/><br/>Cormac McCarthy's "The Border" trilogy is where you'll find dusty plains, hard living, and a recent past populated by a people still living in an even more distant past. His characters are full of character, thei</span>
  <span id="freeText15383686736334337006" style="display:none">One decision, as innocent as it may be, can fuck up your life forever. Now, you can live in fear and hide yourself away, or you can keep making those decisions and hope for the best, and if and when the shit hits the fan, you can stand strong and push on.<br /><br />That's life. That's <i>The Crossing</i>.<br /><br />Cormac McCarthy's "The Border" trilogy is where you'll find dusty plains, hard living, and a recent past populated by a people still living in an even more distant past. His characters are full of character, their own code and a new version of an old set of morals.<br /><br /><i>The Crossing</i> is Homeric. There is a hero with a quest. There is a wise man. There are fools. And there are monsters. The hero's journey plays out upon the border between New Mexico and old Mexico, where the line dividing life and death is measured in handfuls of blood.<br /><br />McCarthy's books are not where you shop for your good times and happy endings. His characters will die and you will feel pain.<br /><br />I spent a good amount of time in early 2014 in southern Mexico. It was a learning experience and it helped me to appreciate what's in this novel. Not only was I able to follow along with much of the Spanish dialogue (it's basic stuff, trust me, I'm not bragging here), but the portrayal of the life and the people rings true and brings to mind images, scenes and people I saw and met during my time in that parched land.<br /><br />I'm giving this five stars, not because I think it's perfect and that everyone will love it. In fact, I think many people would not like this. McCarthy occasionally veers from the action-packed path to discuss life and that irks some readers. However, I give it five stars for McCarthy's writing. It's superb. His language usage...ah, those glorious descriptions! It's all too beautiful!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15383686736334337006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2603334836" />
    <a title="Katie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56035852-katie"><img alt="Katie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469958232p2/56035852.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2603334836?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie" href="/user/show/56035852-katie">Katie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2603334836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15579364030484738569">I did love this but found it slightly less successful than the first book in this trilogy. For one thing it follows an almost identical formula - innocence going out into the big bad world. The opening when a young boy watches wolves at play in the snow at night is magical. It continues in this gripping fashion when the boy takes pity on the wolf he and his father have caught in a trap and decides to take the injured animal back to the mountains of Mexico. The relationship he creates between the</span>
  <span id="freeText15579364030484738569" style="display:none">I did love this but found it slightly less successful than the first book in this trilogy. For one thing it follows an almost identical formula - innocence going out into the big bad world. The opening when a young boy watches wolves at play in the snow at night is magical. It continues in this gripping fashion when the boy takes pity on the wolf he and his father have caught in a trap and decides to take the injured animal back to the mountains of Mexico. The relationship he creates between the boy and the wolf is a marvel in itself. In Mexico he sees his father's horse and knows something bad has happened at home. <br /><br /> People often speak of the violence in his books but what always gets to me is the deep beautiful bonds he establishes between his characters and often between a character and an animal. This is very much true here where the relationship Billy shares with his brother, the wolf, his horse and his dog are all deeply moving. He gets us to care so much for his characters that the possibility of heartbreak looms forebodingly on every turned page. Another thing I love about his books is the philosophy. As I said he's so good at engaging you on a feeling level; then his characters always meet eccentric individuals on their travels whose philosophising make you think about everything that is happening on a deeper more intellectual level. He engages the intellect as challengingly as the emotions. 4.5 stars. And now onto book three…<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15579364030484738569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3837525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3837525" />
    <a title="Joe Briggs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/239610-joe-briggs"><img alt="Joe Briggs" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1185877278p2/239610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3837525?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Briggs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Briggs" href="/user/show/239610-joe-briggs">Joe Briggs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3837525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11771358828083835574">The Crossing is an astonishing book, more downbeat than All the Pretty Horses, yet not as bleak as the likes of Blood Meridian, it is a sprawling coming-of-age tale filled with moments of beauty and sorrow. The descriptions are as beautiful as anything Cormac McCarthy writes, the action is sparse but nailbiting when it comes and the characters are brilliantly realised. There are moments when the book lags but whenever this happens you can be assured that within a couple of pages McCarthy will co</span>
  <span id="freeText11771358828083835574" style="display:none">The Crossing is an astonishing book, more downbeat than All the Pretty Horses, yet not as bleak as the likes of Blood Meridian, it is a sprawling coming-of-age tale filled with moments of beauty and sorrow. The descriptions are as beautiful as anything Cormac McCarthy writes, the action is sparse but nailbiting when it comes and the characters are brilliantly realised. There are moments when the book lags but whenever this happens you can be assured that within a couple of pages McCarthy will come out with a line or paragraph that is so amazing you'll have to reread several times, possibly out loud, before you can continue with the story. The book never gives you all the information about some aspects of the story which is sometimes frustrating but works within the confines of the world that McCarthy is created, he's never one to end everything neatly and perfectly, the subplot of Billy's brother and the girl leaves you wishing for more though is all the more powerful for the fact that it's heard in rumours and secondhand recollections. Throughout the book there are times when Billy will meet characters who will tell them their own story, these digressions act as stories within the main narrative, both separate from it and integral to it. The story of the blind revolutionary in particular is fantastic, as McCarthy drops his visual mastery to explain the blind man's travels. This book is a journey from youth to adulthood, from hope to despair, along a hard path populated with kind hearts and desperate men. This book is a journey that you never want to end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11771358828083835574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6763386165083451128">Alice Munro said in an interview that our lives begin as straightforward stories with the typical arc of fiction, but that as we go on living they become strange, experimental narratives, convoluted and difficult to interpret. It seems to me that's what's happening in this second volume of the Border Trilogy. Volume One was pretty straightforward, taut and clear in its construction. It told a story of a young man's searing introduction to the adult world. Volume Two does the same--with a differe</span>
  <span id="freeText6763386165083451128" style="display:none">Alice Munro said in an interview that our lives begin as straightforward stories with the typical arc of fiction, but that as we go on living they become strange, experimental narratives, convoluted and difficult to interpret. It seems to me that's what's happening in this second volume of the Border Trilogy. Volume One was pretty straightforward, taut and clear in its construction. It told a story of a young man's searing introduction to the adult world. Volume Two does the same--with a different young man--yet its structure defies the conventions of fiction. After a relatively focused (and actually quite wonderful) first 125 pages, the novel circles and digresses for another 300. It tried my patience. I pressed on, though, in the hopes that Volume Three will make good on the promise of the first book and redeem the disappointment of this one.</span>

<div id="review_3266696597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3266696597" />
    <a title="Jessaka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka"><img alt="Jessaka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485709659p2/4659464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3266696597?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessaka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessaka" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka">Jessaka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3266696597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14800193623729245074">Acts of God<br/><br/>“Before he reached the door the old man called to him again. The boy turned and stood. The matrix will not help you, the old man said. He said to catch the wolf the boy should find that place where the acts of God and those of man are of one piece. Where they cannot be distinguished. The old man said that it was not a question of finding such a place but rather of knowing it when it presented itself. He said that it was at such places that God sits and conspires in the destruction of </span>
  <span id="freeText14800193623729245074" style="display:none">Acts of God<br /><br />“Before he reached the door the old man called to him again. The boy turned and stood. The matrix will not help you, the old man said. He said to catch the wolf the boy should find that place where the acts of God and those of man are of one piece. Where they cannot be distinguished. The old man said that it was not a question of finding such a place but rather of knowing it when it presented itself. He said that it was at such places that God sits and conspires in the destruction of that which he has been at such pains to create.”<br /><br />New Mexico. Early 1940s. A wolf had come up from Mexico, killing cattle on a ranch. Billy and his father set out traps to catch it, but the wolf uncovered them all. Then Billy set a trap in the ashes of a cold campfire. The following day when he went alone to check the trap, he had caught the pregnant wolf, her foot badly injured. He took pity on her and tied her up to protect himself from being mauled, even killed. Dragging her on three legs, he headed home. Thinking it over, he took a detour and ended up at a neighboring ranch, where the woman doctored the wolf’s foot, fed the boy, and then the boy went on his way to Mexico, to save the wolf, to release her to others of her kind.<br /><br />I thought a lot about this: What will McCarthy do with his story? The boy was kind-hearted, but was McCarthy? Had McCarthy softened in his old age? I wanted to believe he had. In time the wolf became almost like a companion to Billy, but Billy was naïve. He fed and watered the wolf, he protected her from vicious dogs and man alike. He even saved her from drowning in a river which they had been crossing. And he grew to love her, but maybe he loved her from the moment he saw her in that trap. Maybe, I thought, we are all trapped in one way or another. But McCarthy, being true to his own nature, couldn’t allow it to be. Even today, it is hard to imagine a wolf surviving in our world, a world that hates the wolf. Then it began happening, and it wouldn’t stop. In the end, do we praise McCarthy for his prose, or do we curse him for his cruelty? I did both. Then I put the book down, but only for the night and part of the following day.<br /><br />The boy headed back to New Mexico, to his father’s ranch. He was growing up fast at the young age of 17. When he arrived at the ranch, things had changed. He got his brother Boyd, and together they went to Mexico to find the horses that had been stolen from his father’s ranch. Some things should be left alone, forgotten. Maybe even forgiven. <br /><br />“It had ceased raining in the night and he walked out on the road and called for the dog. He called and called. Standing in that inexplicable darkness when there was no sound anywhere save only the wind. After a while he sat in the road. He took off his hat and placed it on the tarmac before him and he bowed his head and held his face in his hands and wept. He sat there for a long time and after a while the east did gray and after a while the right and the godmade sun did rise, once again, for all and without distinction.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="14800193623729245074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3313440981" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3313440981" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18218857-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362876012p2/18218857.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3313440981?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/18218857-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3313440981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16087905449435071481">I'm not sure what I expected going into this read, but I certainly didn't expect such a radiant, brutal, multi-faceted experience. For me, it succeeded on so many levels. Often spartan with his language, McCarthy is exacting with its impact. A spur-of-the-moment decision sends young Billy into Mexico and creates a kind of Bildungsroman-as-lamentation. Every stranger he encounters is like an oracle in disguise. Some deliver advice. Some wish to deliver death. A world where hospitality and danger </span>
  <span id="freeText16087905449435071481" style="display:none">I'm not sure what I expected going into this read, but I certainly didn't expect such a radiant, brutal, multi-faceted experience. For me, it succeeded on so many levels. Often spartan with his language, McCarthy is exacting with its impact. A spur-of-the-moment decision sends young Billy into Mexico and creates a kind of Bildungsroman-as-lamentation. Every stranger he encounters is like an oracle in disguise. Some deliver advice. Some wish to deliver death. A world where hospitality and danger intermingle continuously.<br /><br />And the prose floats between stark reality and the sublime: <blockquote>“The movement of figures in the room slowed, the low mutterings of the condolent died to a whisper. The mourners wished one another that they profit from their meal and then all of it ground away in the history of its own repetition and he could hear those antecedent ceremonies dropping somewhere like wooden blocks into their slots. Like tumblers in a lock or like the wooden gearteeth in old machinery slipping one by one into the mortices cut in the cogwheel rolling up to meet them.”</blockquote><br />Grounded in reality, it's like a Western elevated to allegory. An ode to lost ways, lost lives, and the perpetual grind of history. The characters resonate with an abundance of depth by way of a few short lines breathing life through facial expression, postures, terse dialogue.<br /><br />Damn.<br />---------------------------------<br />THIS IS PROBABLY 2/3 OF THE WORDS I DIDN'T KNOW IN THIS BOOK<br />fumarole | vaqueros | la almohada | siéntate | contumacious | alcahest | ocotillo | bosal | lechuguilla | arrieros | kiacks | esclarajo | serranos | pozole | bier | mozo | alguacil | cobarde | agárrala | chozas | wickiups | huérfano | majoneras | celadon | terremoto | vigas | moren | consanguinity | quoined | jinete | canebrake | remuda | scaup | selvedge | demiculverin | ciborium | indenominate | ejiditarios | latifundio | gorgios | caliche | preterite<br />---------------------------------<br />HANDY READING RESOURCE<br />Translations of all the Spanish in this book: <a href="http://www.cormacmccarthy.com/wp-content/uploads/CrossingTrans.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">http://www.cormacmccarthy.com/wp-content/uploads/CrossingTrans.pdf</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16087905449435071481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=north-america">north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=storytelling">storytelling</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=nature1">nature1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=journeys">journeys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=animals">animals</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3402502090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13731133765459429979"><i>The Crossing</i> is a coming of age story set in the late 1930’s and early 1940’s near the US-Mexican border. It is the second book in McCarthy’s “Border Trilogy,” but may be read as a standalone. It is broken into three sections, with each correlated to one of Billy’s three quests.<br/><br/>As the book opens, teenage protagonist Billy lives with his parents and brother on the family’s isolated ranch in the New Mexican desert. He makes three trips across the border, involving a wolf, the family’s horses, and </span>
  <span id="freeText13731133765459429979" style="display:none"><i>The Crossing</i> is a coming of age story set in the late 1930’s and early 1940’s near the US-Mexican border. It is the second book in McCarthy’s “Border Trilogy,” but may be read as a standalone. It is broken into three sections, with each correlated to one of Billy’s three quests.<br /><br />As the book opens, teenage protagonist Billy lives with his parents and brother on the family’s isolated ranch in the New Mexican desert. He makes three trips across the border, involving a wolf, the family’s horses, and a missing person. None of these quests turns out as planned. During his travels, he gains wisdom through his experiences and discussions with local sages. Themes include guilt, fate, heroism, and the desire to live an honorable life (which he finds to be harder than it sounds). <br /><br />The tone is dark. Billy suffers many hardships. His motivations are not always apparent. Sometimes he just “decides to do something” without thinking it through, and the consequences are dire. <br /><br />McCarthy leaves many Spanish words and phrases (and a few paragraphs) untranslated, so passable knowledge of Spanish is helpful. If you are not a Spanish reader, you may want to keep a translation tool handy. I feel this book is a worthy follow-up to the first book in this series, <i>All the Pretty Horses,</i> which I also enjoyed and recommend. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13731133765459429979" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1623520950" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1623520950" />
    <a title="Wayne Barrett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21372390-wayne-barrett"><img alt="Wayne Barrett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601067617p2/21372390.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wayne Barrett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wayne Barrett" href="/user/show/21372390-wayne-barrett">Wayne Barrett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=series">series</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1623520950" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5386139225708822650"><br/>Part II of The Border Trilogy. This wasn't nearly as good as 'All The Pretty Horses', but it was still a powerful novel... then again, why wouldn't it be? It's Cormack!<br/>From his home in New Mexico, young Billy Parham decides to take a wild wolf that has been trapped and set it free in its faraway home in Mexico. Billy succeeds in setting the lobo free, but not like he intended. Because at that juncture there was an unseen obstacle in his path... there was 'A Crossing'. <br/>I think the title is fittin</span>
  <span id="freeText5386139225708822650" style="display:none"><br />Part II of The Border Trilogy. This wasn't nearly as good as 'All The Pretty Horses', but it was still a powerful novel... then again, why wouldn't it be? It's Cormack!<br />From his home in New Mexico, young Billy Parham decides to take a wild wolf that has been trapped and set it free in its faraway home in Mexico. Billy succeeds in setting the lobo free, but not like he intended. Because at that juncture there was an unseen obstacle in his path... there was 'A Crossing'. <br />I think the title is fitting for the story. In the gritty style that McCarthy is so famous for, he takes Billy and his brother Boyd on a journey, where just as in real life, there are hurtles, bridges and rivers to cross, forks in the road where a decision to go left or right could be the difference in the outcome of your entire life. <br />Billy makes this particular journey, imprinting a lifetime of memories and unforgettable experiences while he is still in his teens. And in the end... well, there is no end here. There is no dramatic flair, no unexpected twists, no blooming romance. It's just another place in his life... it just is. Another crossing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5386139225708822650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3083699520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3083699520" />
    <a title="Bob Brinkmeyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34401683-bob-brinkmeyer"><img alt="Bob Brinkmeyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580600423p2/34401683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3083699520?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Brinkmeyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Brinkmeyer" href="/user/show/34401683-bob-brinkmeyer">Bob Brinkmeyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3083699520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16765503385582684506">The second volume of Cormac McCarthy’s magisterial Border Trilogy, The Crossing, introduces one of McCarthy’s most intriguing characters, Billy Parham, a teenager living with his family on a ranch in New Mexico in the 1940s. The novel opens with a haunting scene when an Indian meets up with Billy and his younger brother Boyd and asks them to retrieve food for him from their family’s house. Billy agrees, but the scene is loaded with tension and danger—and Billy’s act of generosity sets in motion </span>
  <span id="freeText16765503385582684506" style="display:none">The second volume of Cormac McCarthy’s magisterial Border Trilogy, The Crossing, introduces one of McCarthy’s most intriguing characters, Billy Parham, a teenager living with his family on a ranch in New Mexico in the 1940s. The novel opens with a haunting scene when an Indian meets up with Billy and his younger brother Boyd and asks them to retrieve food for him from their family’s house. Billy agrees, but the scene is loaded with tension and danger—and Billy’s act of generosity sets in motion a chain of events that will forever alter his, Boyd’s, and his parents’ lives.<br /><br />Most of the first third of the book concerns Billy’s trapping of a wolf that is feeding on cattle and his seemingly incomprehensible decision to nurse the injured wolf back to health and return it to the wild in Mexico, from where it had come. As crazy and ill-guided as Billy might seem (and a few people he meets tell him just that), there’s a goodness and a sense of justice deep within him, core attributes that, given the darkness of McCarthy’s world, doesn’t always bode well for Billy. That doesn’t mean Billy’s goodness is not worthy and commendable; indeed, it’s generally small acts of kindness rather than grand exploits that most searchingly signal the quiet heroism of McCarthy’s heroes. Such heroism can be seen most forthrightly in the actions of a doctor Billy seeks out (in a later trip into Mexico) to treat the gravely injured Boyd. Awakened in the middle of the night, the doctor agrees without hesitation to make the journey to Boyd; there he carefully cleans and treats the boy’s wound, in a scene presented in painstaking detail and stretching close to 10 pages, a scene underscoring the doctor’s skill and his commitment to his craft—and his fundamental goodness. It’s one of the most powerfully presented scenes in all of McCarthy. The doctor, I should add, asks for nothing in return for his work. <br /><br />Billy’s journey with the wolf is one of three crossings into Mexico that he undertakes in the novel. All involve righting an injustice and restoring a sense of order; none ends well. At the end, Billy is back on the road, a wanderer, without a place, without a home. It’s clear he would be better off if he settled down. But where? As underscored throughout the novel, the West that Billy has grown up knowing is rapidly being transformed by modernization and militarization. The old ways of traditional ranch life are passing. Near the end of the novel, Billy meets another wanderer who says to him: “This world will never be the same. Did you know that?” Billy answers simply: “I know it. It aint now.” <br /><br />One final observation: McCarthy’s prose, as always, is masterful, if not unequalled. There are so many passages I could quote, but here’s only one, a single sentence--simple, spare, and haunting--describing a moment of interaction between Billy and the wolf: “She watched him with her yellow eyes and in them was no despair but only the reckonless deep of loneliness that cored the world to its heart.” <br /><br />This a great book. And let me add, in case you don’t know, Billy’s story, as well as that of John Grady Cole, the protagonist of All the Pretty Horses, continues in the final volume of the Border Trilogy, Cities of the Plain.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16765503385582684506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1904395606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1904395606" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1904395606?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330511247" href="/book/show/7069817-the-crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1904395606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14210297236786497151">‘The Crossing’ (1994) – is the second part in Cormac McCarthy’s monumental, much revered and critically acclaimed border trilogy (preceded by ‘All The Pretty Horses’ and followed by ‘Cities of The Plain’).<br/><br/>‘The Crossing’ tells the story of one Billy Parham and opens with Billy’s attempted quest to return a trapped and injured wolf to Mexico from whence it came. This is a dark, powerful and poetic book and it is hard to capture or encapsulate the essence of ‘The Crossing’ any better than the quote</span>
  <span id="freeText14210297236786497151" style="display:none">‘The Crossing’ (1994) – is the second part in Cormac McCarthy’s monumental, much revered and critically acclaimed border trilogy (preceded by ‘All The Pretty Horses’ and followed by ‘Cities of The Plain’).<br /><br />‘The Crossing’ tells the story of one Billy Parham and opens with Billy’s attempted quest to return a trapped and injured wolf to Mexico from whence it came. This is a dark, powerful and poetic book and it is hard to capture or encapsulate the essence of ‘The Crossing’ any better than the quote on the book cover – “Like a river in full spate – beautiful and dangerous” (The Times).<br /><br />As with the other parts of McCarthy’s border trilogy, ‘The Crossing’ is set on the New Mexico/Mexico border, this time during the 1930s/40s, yet despite its’ relative historical proximity – this is a world which often here feels very alien, very different, it’s a different world, a different landscape, a different people and different ways of life – yet despite such differences, this is a very relatable story, it feels very up close and personal. <br /><br />This is a book that tells us a long, engaging and powerful story – perhaps even stronger than its’ predecessor ‘All The Pretty Horses’ – ‘The Crossing’ is very much a quest, it is an existential, elemental journey on an almost biblical scale. At times ‘Crossing’ is almost dreamlike and often hints or even references ‘Don Quixote’ (Cervantes). McCarthy gives us powerful prose, darkly poetic, grimly majestic – this is in some respects, a post (personal) apocalypse novel, quasi dystopian. <br /><br />Utterly absorbing and strongly recommended. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14210297236786497151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_125513953" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125513953" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605586833p2/869064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125513953?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=cormac-mccarthy">cormac-mccarthy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125513953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11971301562618653653">Comparisons between this and All the Pretty Horses seem inevitable. Here we have another buldingsroman: a teenage cowboy who rides south into the Mexican frontier, coming of age through scenes of privation and violence. But Billy Parham's journey has a a peculiarly mystical quality all its own. He keeps meeting these extremely odd people out in the wilderness who feel the need to explain to him, in deliriously long, wide-ranging monologues, their gnostically inclined ideas of God, History, Man, </span>
  <span id="freeText11971301562618653653" style="display:none">Comparisons between this and All the Pretty Horses seem inevitable. Here we have another buldingsroman: a teenage cowboy who rides south into the Mexican frontier, coming of age through scenes of privation and violence. But Billy Parham's journey has a a peculiarly mystical quality all its own. He keeps meeting these extremely odd people out in the wilderness who feel the need to explain to him, in deliriously long, wide-ranging monologues, their gnostically inclined ideas of God, History, Man, Fate, what have you. But these weird confessions seem somehow necessary, since in between them, in the main narrative, this kid loses EVERYTHING that ties him to the world. That's what is so weird about this. It's utterly brutal, but it's got this really rich, contemplative spiritual dimension which is so often chocked out by the human cruelty in Mccarthy's work. It's got a powerfully redemptive quality which I've never come across in his writing before, almost in spite of itself. Note: Tons of the dialogue is in Spanish, and it really helps to have a bi-lingual dictionary by you're side when your going through it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11971301562618653653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/54803799?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="trickgnosis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="trickgnosis" href="/user/show/1266214-trickgnosis">trickgnosis</a>
      </span>

        rated it

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103268861?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/3747371-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3747371-kathryn?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3747371-kathryn?shelf=western">western</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103268861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14497494782433236909">The second novel in McCarthy’s Border Trilogy is not a sequel to All the Pretty Horses, but rather a parallel coming-of-age story. Billy Parham is a sixteen year old boy living on a ranch in New Mexico. When a wolf suddenly begins killing his family’s cattle, Billy’s father sets him with the task of trapping the wolf. A game of wits between boy and beast ensues, as the wolf repeatedly digs up the traps that have been set for her. When Billy finally outsmarts the wolf and catches her, he feels su</span>
  <span id="freeText14497494782433236909" style="display:none">The second novel in McCarthy’s Border Trilogy is not a sequel to All the Pretty Horses, but rather a parallel coming-of-age story. Billy Parham is a sixteen year old boy living on a ranch in New Mexico. When a wolf suddenly begins killing his family’s cattle, Billy’s father sets him with the task of trapping the wolf. A game of wits between boy and beast ensues, as the wolf repeatedly digs up the traps that have been set for her. When Billy finally outsmarts the wolf and catches her, he feels such a bond with her that he doesn’t have the heart to kill her. Instead, he sets out to cross the border into Mexico, returning the wolf to the country whence she came. His life-altering trek eventually leads him back home, where he realizes that his journey has just begun.<br /><br />This highly literary tale will appeal to fans of complex westerns like Deadwood, as well as McCarthy’s other works – and can be read as a standalone novel. Readers returning to the trilogy seeking another romantic tale like All the Pretty Horses should be forewarned, however: this installment is much bleaker than its predecessor. The first part of the novel is compelling and, in the end, heartwrenching; a haunting story that could easily stand on its own – and indeed is the saving grace of the entire book. But from there, as Billy makes his second and third crossings into Mexico, the plot devolves into a series of ponderous meanderings that lacks the same emotional payoff that characterized the first portion of the novel. For this reviewer, the novel was not rewarding enough to justify the challenge of reading it – but others may be up to the task.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14497494782433236909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6554430" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6554430" />
    <a title="MikeS" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/398987-mikes"><img alt="MikeS" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277679356p2/398987.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6554430?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MikeS" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MikeS" href="/user/show/398987-mikes">MikeS</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679760849" href="/book/show/744388.The_Crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/398987-mikes?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/398987-mikes?shelf=mimetic-fiction">mimetic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/398987-mikes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6554430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4152660177209403545">After finishing <i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, I felt (maybe somewhat unjustly) that the bar of expectation had been set extremely high. I realize that some (most?) people have a particular favorite part of McCarthy’s Border Trilogy, but I’d be hard-pressed to choose one after reading <i>The Crossing</i>. However, the change was noticeable and I was relieved that the second book wasn’t just a rehashing of the first in theme and tone. <i>The Crossing</i> does maintain the elegant, sprawling prose contrasted by McCarth</span>
  <span id="freeText4152660177209403545" style="display:none">After finishing <i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, I felt (maybe somewhat unjustly) that the bar of expectation had been set extremely high. I realize that some (most?) people have a particular favorite part of McCarthy’s Border Trilogy, but I’d be hard-pressed to choose one after reading <i>The Crossing</i>. However, the change was noticeable and I was relieved that the second book wasn’t just a rehashing of the first in theme and tone. <i>The Crossing</i> does maintain the elegant, sprawling prose contrasted by McCarthy’s characteristically minimalist dialog, but utilizes the contextual similarities to other, darker purposes. That is to say, McCarthy doesn’t pull any punches, which I think is why these books resist predictability – he goes to the Bad Place and then some, but it’s always in a plausible, if tragic way. <br />If <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> is a treatise on Love and Beauty (which I would argue it is), then <i>The Crossing</i> is an opus that dissects the mortal crises over Spirituality and Death. The narrative thread here is also more ambiguous than <i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, revealing itself like unmarked sequences of a dream, colorful and haunting, bleak and inevitable. The evident symbolism of “crossing” back and forth over the U.S./Mexico border segues into metaphysical possibilities and issues rather than merely being a one-dimensional metaphor by which to cheaply entertain. What begins as a simple journey ultimately becomes a Lynchian quest for cathartic realization of Self and Other, brought together through blood. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4152660177209403545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1546840022" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1546840022?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330511247" href="/book/show/7069817-the-crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1546840022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15313131525071930885">This is the third consecutive Cormac McCarthy novel I've read, and I'm about to start a fourth. So at this point I'm convinced that he is an outstanding writer - the only point I'm undecided on is whether <i>The Crossing</i> gets 4 stars or 5 stars. <br/><br/>I did feel that the storyline was a little loose when compared to <i>All The Pretty Horses</i>. A few times the story felt like it completey wrapped up, but then it kind of kept going, awkwardly finding a new direction. The book really reads like three separate st</span>
  <span id="freeText15313131525071930885" style="display:none">This is the third consecutive Cormac McCarthy novel I've read, and I'm about to start a fourth. So at this point I'm convinced that he is an outstanding writer - the only point I'm undecided on is whether <i>The Crossing</i> gets 4 stars or 5 stars. <br /><br />I did feel that the storyline was a little loose when compared to <i>All The Pretty Horses</i>. A few times the story felt like it completey wrapped up, but then it kind of kept going, awkwardly finding a new direction. The book really reads like three separate stories about a single character, wrapped up with some overarching themes and ideas. This format felt a little odd when reading it, but I don't necessarily want to hold this structure against the book - it's not important that a novel fits a standard format or structure. And by the end you get an idea of what McCarthy may be trying to do with the format. After all, a person's life doesn't really fit a neat narrative structure - it can have many phases which may not have very much to do with each other except for distant cause and effect and the fact that they are tied to that person. I think that McCarthy likes to play with these kinds of reflections about the nature of life.<br /><br />Despite the meandering, I never felt bored with this book. I get the feeling that McCarthy could write a story about anything and it would be totally real and compelling. Really, the writing is about as good as writing gets. You don't have to read for very long before coming across a subtly wonderful phrase, or a tense scene, or a interesting moral choice, or a piece of writing that succinctly illuminates an aspect of the human condition. And it's all wrapped up in such direct language - we don't get inside the characters' heads - we just see them act and hear the words they speak and through their words and deeds we get to know them and understand them and understand ourselves and the world.<br /><br />I think I've talked myself into a rating.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15313131525071930885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
