    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_520232776" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/520232776" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1716113-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1398205239p2/1716113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/520232776?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/1716113-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1716113-mark?shelf=read-back2library-who-cares">read-back2library-who-cares</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer520232776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10276339341103700204">Judging from the four—closing in on five—Cormac McCarthy novels I've read so far, he is not a writer eager to share an abundant sense of humor with his readers. So the folksy (and very funny) joke an elder fellow cattle-rancher tells to <i>The Crossing</i>’s protagonist Billy Parham less than fifty pages into the more than 400 page novel is a rarity, if not a singularity:<br/><blockquote>There was this Texas lion and this New Mexico lion. They split up on the divide and went off to hunt. Agreed to meet up in the spring</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10276339341103700204" style="display:none">Judging from the four—closing in on five—Cormac McCarthy novels I've read so far, he is not a writer eager to share an abundant sense of humor with his readers. So the folksy (and very funny) joke an elder fellow cattle-rancher tells to <i>The Crossing</i>’s protagonist Billy Parham less than fifty pages into the more than 400 page novel is a rarity, if not a singularity:<br /><blockquote>There was this Texas lion and this New Mexico lion. They split up on the divide and went off to hunt. Agreed to meet up in the spring and see how they done and all and whenever they done it why the old lion been over in Texas looked just awful. Lion from New Mexico he looked at him and he said lord son you look awful. Said what's happened to you. Lion's been over in Texas said I don't know. Said I'm about starved out. Other old lion said well, said tell me what all you been doin. Said you might be doin somethin wrong.<br /><br />Well the Texas lion said I just been usin the old tried and true methods. Said I get up on a limb overlookin the trail and then whenever one of the Texans ride underneath it why I holler real big and then I jump out on top of him. And that's what I been a doin.<br /><br />Well, the old New Mexico lion looked at him and said it's a wonder you aint dead. Said that's all wrong for your Texans and I dont see how you got through the winter atall. Said look here. First of all when you holler thataway it scares the shit out of em. Then when you jump on top of them thataway it knocks the wind out of em. Hell, son. You aint got nothing left but buckles and boots.</blockquote><br /><br />That's a concentrated dose of one thing McCarthy does very well: create believable colloquial regional diction so finely honed that, even if you are unfamiliar with the patois in question, it so vividly lodges in your mind that you intuitively accept it as airtight accurate.<br /><br />It's also is an example of something McCarthy doesn’t do often enough: use one of his strong skills as a writer to make an unequivocal point or paint an unambiguous picture of human behavior. The old rancher who told the joke resides, as does Billy Parham, in New Mexico. Obviously McCarthy wants to convey that some New Mexican folks in the time this novel is set—half a dozen years or so leading up to America’s entry into World War II—disdained Texans. McCarthy skillfully extends that notion—as well as the humor—beyond the joke told as the old man, continuing the conversation once he regains his composure from cracking himself up, says<br /> <br /><blockquote>You see the point? he said. Texans?<br />Billy smiled. Yessir, he said.<br />You aint from Texas are you?<br />No sir.<br />I didn’t allow you was.</blockquote><br /><br />I think that’s really magnificent writing. But then there are all the other 400 and some odd pages of this novel, which I find it difficult to think of the same way.<br /><br />After reading the "novel entire" (placement of that adjective after, rather than before, the noun it modifies is a device McCarthy uses several times in this book), I didn’t encounter a single passage anywhere else that fired my imagination and staid with me the way the above excerpt did. In fact, if we accept the old man’s joke as true, I’d half suspect McCarthy of being Texan himself if I didn’t know better.<br /><br />For me all of McCarthy’s elsewhere critically acclaimed descriptions of the early twentieth century American Southwest and Mexican landscapes, his overwrought and oddly non sequitur figurative language and the frequently unfathomable actions of his characters add up to nothing more than a wind that blows mighty hot and bears no solid narrative integrity my way.<br /><br />If that’s not bad enough, <i>The Crossing</i>’s length as a novel stretches out at two different places for several clunky, lengthy philosophical interludes (I don’t know what else you’d call them) which amount to that thing other than wind that the hungry wolf from the joke inadvertently purged from the Texans he preyed upon.<br /><br />Let’s take the convoluted metaphors and twisted similes first. After much trial and error, our young hero Billy Parham has successfully trapped the wolf that has been killing his rancher family’s livestock. Instead of shooting the wolf dead while it’s caught in the trap, as his father instructed, he decides—imbued with some unexplained sixth sense that now is the time to embark upon a quixotic coming-of-age quest—to take the wolf as a living captive and return it to the Mexican mountains. In a scene where a series of events have brought Billy to a point where he can allow the otherwise muzzled wolf to drink some much needed water, we find him “squatted there watching her (the wolf) with rope in both hands. Like a man entrusted with the keeping of something which he hardly knew the use of.”<br /><br />Well, he’s not <i>like</i> such a man, he <i>IS</i>, at that precise moment, “a man entrusted (by his own volition) with the keeping of something which he hardly knew the use of.” At several times he as much as admits that he’s not quite sure what his motivation is for trying to take the wolf back to the mountains, so he hardly knows the “use” of her or his mission. To my ears the simile sounds too much in the vein of—to quote a satire of lame prose from a comedy television show I no longer remember the name of—“the brick hit the ground like a heavy object falling to earth.”<br /><br />In later dealings with the wolf (I’ll spare the details to avoid a spoiler), Billy hoists a wet sheet “in a trestlepole” close to an open fire he’s set whereupon the sheet “steamed in the firelight like a burning scrim standing in a wilderness where celebrants of some sacred passion had been carried off by rival sects or perhaps had simply fled in the night at the fear of their own doing.” Now read that passage over again and try to picture in your mind’s eyes how exactly, or even approximately, that steam looked. When what’s on the other side of a simile’s equation is more elaborate, abstract and visually ambiguous than the thing attempting to be described, what is the point of using a simile?<br /><br />McCarthy writes wince-inducing clamshell figurations like this with such frequency that these two examples don’t do justice to how silly they can be. I half wish I had kept better track of them because at this writing I can recall the existence but not the substance of one or two doozies that make the above examples seem like slight offenses. And if you suspect me of being picayune or of reading the book to actively try and find fault, my defense is that when McCarthy bombed with these twisted tropes, he wrenched me from what little forward momentum his fiction compelled me to and honestly, once or twice, I paused in my reading to seriously consider whether he wasn’t just trying to pull my leg.<br /><br />I’m not alone in noticing this weird trait of McCarthy’s prose. James Wood, in his review of <i>No Country for Old Men</i> in <i>The New Yorker</i>, observes McCarthy's "fondness for what could be called analogical similes, in which the linking phrase 'like some' introduces not a visual likeness but a hypothetical and often abstract parallel: 'And he went forth stained and stinking like some reeking issue of the incarnate dam of war herself.'" (this passage, by the way, is from McCarthy’s much ballyhooed <i>Blood Meridian</i>).<br /><br />Wood’s conclusion about McCarthy’s grandiose prose epilepsies is that “the danger is not just melodrama but imprecision and, occasionally, something close to nonsense.” After reading <i>The Crossing</i> I’d say “close to” amounts to Wood politely pulling his punch.<br /><br />But what of the novel’s guts—the story that McCarthy is telling and the characters who move through it and make it happen? If the undergirding here is a captivating story, couldn’t we dismiss the excess of “analogical similes” as annoying but easily ignored doilies draped over an otherwise well constructed chair? McCarthy comes up short here, too. The first section of the book is the aforementioned boy-traps-she-wolf-and-falls-in-love (or at least in “quest”) saga, followed by what seems like half a dozen false starts toward something never realized. If McCarthy intended to follow, update or even stand on its head the picaresque novel tradition—an idea Robert Hass flirts with in his slobberingly venerating <i>The New York Times</i> review of <i>The Crossing</i>—why tack what might as well be a novella at the beginning? Why not be more episodic and forego sustaining storylines—at the book’s opening and elsewhere—that are long enough to dupe the reader into thinking that it all might actually be going somewhere?<br /><br />As was the case with John Grady Cole, the main character of <i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, I spent plenty of fruitless time pondering what made Billy Parham tick. Why, seemingly suddenly, was it so vitally important for him to return that she-wolf to the mountains of Mexico? The closest McCarthy brings us to even a hint of what might be going on inside Parham’s head was when the boy speaks to himself at what I imagine could be the pivotal point where he decides to undertake his mission: “By damn, he said. By damn.” The few times we encounter Billy Parham’s father it’s made clear that he doesn’t put up with any foolishness and that the son has respect for the father and the father is appropriately parental to the son. Yet when Billy heads out one day with his father fully aware that he’s going to see whether or not they’ve trapped a dangerous wolf and doesn’t return for days, there’s not so much as a search party mentioned. Even the distant neighbor who recognizes him as “Will Parham’s boy” decides to feed him, give his captive she-wolf pet some first aid and send him on his way with some supplies and no moral misgivings about not telling Billy’s father. You can just picture the neighbor grimly smoking his cigarette, watching Billy ride off in to the sunset with his lupine captive and saying to his wife, “I reckon he just needs to follow through on his own mythological rite of passage journey with that damned wolf. I think we have to kindly abandon what real and believable people would do in this here sitcheeaashun.”<br /><br />Sporadically punching up (not) this hard to swallow stuff are the aforementioned philosophical interludes. The first one is the thoroughly digestible, even enjoyable, appetizer of Billy visiting Don Arnulfo, a knocking on death’s door Mexican trapper, who proffers wisdom to the young boy who has not yet trapped his quarry, explaining how the wolf is simultaneously “a being of a greater order … that . . . knows what men do not” and “like a snowflake.” This seems worse than it is. The “conversation entire” is held to only four pages or so and the ideas expressed are fairly clear and straightforward: the wolf belongs to the cosmic order of nature to which humans are just as likely to be an aberration as above and trying to contain any element of that world will only reveal it as transient and perishable when constrained if only for observation, like a snowflake. I get it.<br /><br />Later on, however, Billy meets an elderly man who relays the tale of the towns Caborca and Huisiachepic and, a little after that, he meets a man gruesomely blinded long ago who relates his own story. Both these sections reel on for over a dozen pages and if I could even begin to figure out what the hell McCarthy is trying to convey through these stilted and tediously repetitious pseudoparables, I’d suggest that he could have accomplished it in a lot less time. But I am without any reasonable idea.<br /><br />I’m baffled that some reviewers put McCarthy—one assumes with a straight face—in the same camp as Shakespeare and Faulkner, but these faux philosophical prattling from cameo characters—and really the cumulative effect of the book on me as a reader—made me wonder if the connection isn’t from a line Shakespeare wrote and Faulkner used for a novel’s title. <i>The Crossing</i> all too often seemed to be “a tale told by an idiot, full of sound and fury, signifying nothing.” And after I read that one joke and enjoyed a few other moments of sparse, grimly humorous dialogue, there was not much left but buckles and boots.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10276339341103700204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_96960247" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96960247" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2447803-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246308632p2/2447803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96960247?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/2447803-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679760849" href="/book/show/744388.The_Crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96960247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11587943066305741375">This is a campfire tale about the humble genesis of a teenage Bad Ass cowboy from the desert southwest. <br/> <br/>The story has 4 parts, each beginning and ending with a US-Mexico border crossing (hence the title). The crossing of the border takes Billy, the main character, a 16 year old boy, from a stark world of American reality to an almost dreamy, magical world of Mexican legend. There's a transcendental relationship between Billy and the earth & elements when he's south of the border. The relationsh</span>
  <span id="freeText11587943066305741375" style="display:none">This is a campfire tale about the humble genesis of a teenage Bad Ass cowboy from the desert southwest. <br /> <br />The story has 4 parts, each beginning and ending with a US-Mexico border crossing (hence the title). The crossing of the border takes Billy, the main character, a 16 year old boy, from a stark world of American reality to an almost dreamy, magical world of Mexican legend. There's a transcendental relationship between Billy and the earth &amp; elements when he's south of the border. The relationship is instinctual and provident. McCarthy describes the environment in exquisite detail...a good campsite, a long-awaited meal, a horse drinking from pooled water, the appearance of the sky. Character actions are bold; dialogue scarce. The narrative is simple but rich, like a symphony made only of woodwinds and kettle drums.<br /><br />In each part, there are a dozen or so pages that remind me of magic realism. The main character meets a strange, but key, individual that suddenly dominates the narrative, and relates in profound language a bizarre experience about the meaning of life. Together, they fathom the esoteric meaning of God. The meaning is ponderous and brilliant, and there is a tenuous silken line of reasoning that threads the 4 Parts together like a master cloth. Although the language is simple, McCarthy takes you on a religious ride, segments of which you may have to re-read. I don't normally re-read long segments of writing, but McCarthy absolutely warped my mind back around itself, very much like a gordian knot, yet somehow my brain worked it out, reasoning--instead of luck--being the better part of challenging the unknown.<br /><br />Cormac McCarthy has done it again in this second installment of the Border Trilogy! He writes like a Buddhist is trained to think: <br />1. simple--yet with profound sumps of meaning like deeper and deeper catacombs <br />2. spartan--yet with a particular economized flair of embellishment<br />3. direct--yet with a lilting poetry of verbiage<br />4. repetitive--yet with a purposeful layering of key points like an alluvial sediment<br />5. exact--yet with time and space to plumb the meaning of God<br /><br />The writing is not the vehicle of the story, it <i>is</i> the story. You may understandably think the story is set to low gear, but you cannot deny that the story is a ne plus ultra of organic, breathing prose. Words matter to McCarthy, and the way they're put together means as much as the action they're used to convey. This author cherishes language. I envision McCarthy weighing, balancing, and judging every word to ensure it reads well, works best with other words, and even looks good on the page. His words are the zootic clay that captures the story like fossils. His sentences are rich and tactile, like a wooden fence, he can drive his thumbnail into it and see the imprint. I almost feel dusty and thirsty after an hour of reading.<br /><br />His prose is also loaded with Spanish words and sentences, sometimes with a glancing translation, and sometimes with no translator help whatsoever. McCarthy will just as boldly use a Spanish word as an English word. He ultimately uses the language that most correctly captures the definition of the noun he seeks. During character conversation in Spanish, the dialogue is so staccato, so simple and short, that even a non-Spanish speaker follows the gist of McCarthy's sentences. McCarthy achieves a beautiful balance between English and Spanish. At times it's romantic, blunt, bountiful, earthy, and achieves a common consanguinity between the two.<br /><br />His writing also uses an adjectivenoun mash-up to great poetic effect. It's different, but it works throughout, and it's highly readable. Same with quotation marks--there are none throughout, and yet it works to streamline the narrative. He avoids punctuation. His longer sentences are composites of shorter sentences with no commas. Examples:<br /><br /><i>He went into the pantry and found some canned peaches and he stood in the dark at the sink eating them out of a glass jar with a cookingspoon (sic) and looking out through the window at the pastureland (sic) to the south blue and silent under the rising moon and the fence running out into the darkness under the mountains and the shadow of the fence crossing the land in the moonlight like a suture.</i> (p. 164)<br /><br /><i>They ate and unrolled their beds and turned in to sleep. The wind blew all night. It burned up the fire and burned up the coals of the fire and the balled and twisted shape of redhot (sic) wire burned briefly like the incandescent armature of an enormous heart in the night's darkness and then faded to black and the wind blew the coals to ash and blew the ash away and scoured the clay where coals and ash had been till other than the blackened wire there was no trace of fire at all and all night things passed in the dark that had of themselves no articulation yet had a destination for that.</i> (p. 172)<br /><br /><i>They sorted out the horses and drove them across the river and set out upcountry. The plains about them blue and devoid of life. The thin horned moon lay on its back in the west like a grail and the bright shape of Venus hung directly above it like a star falling into a boat. They kept to the open country clear of the river and they rode all night and toward the morning they made a dry camp in a quemada of burned trees clustered dead and black and ragged on a slight rise a mile west of the river. They dismounted and looked for some sign of water but there was none.</i> (p. 264)<br /><br />Why not 5 stars? Despite the wolf hunt, murdered parents, outrunning bandidos, getting shot, and the delicate, skilled maneuvering of an American cabellaro in the north Mexican desert, <i>The Crossing</i> lacks just enough critical action to prevent me from awarding an additional star. When I read literature, I can endure meager action and terse dialogue, but there is still a small component of my body that needs some Hollywood moments. Not a lot, but something that digs the spurs deep in my craw.<br /><br />New words: alcahest, kiack, pannier, withy, consanguinity, crone, glaucous</span>
  <a data-text-id="11587943066305741375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13370887540627285716">Cormac McCarthy novels are proof that words are just as powerful, or arguably more so, than images. Reading The Crossing, I lost a little piece of my sanity as I traversed Mexican plains with Billy, the ubiquitous dust drying my mouth. I gazed into the eyes of a starving, pregnant wolf. I caressed tired horses and drank the mescal.<br/><br/>The storytellers who captivated Billy enthralled me.   <span id="freeText13370887540627285716" style="display:none">Cormac McCarthy novels are proof that words are just as powerful, or arguably more so, than images. Reading The Crossing, I lost a little piece of my sanity as I traversed Mexican plains with Billy, the ubiquitous dust drying my mouth. I gazed into the eyes of a starving, pregnant wolf. I caressed tired horses and drank the mescal.<br /><br />The storytellers who captivated Billy enthralled me. I saw the flickers in their eyes and the slight motions of their hands as they created a world in my mind. As my Oklahoman friend Sam would say: The Crossing will give you a "case of the plains," but you won't regret it.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_139769557" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139769557" />
    <a title="Chloe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70078-chloe"><img alt="Chloe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580564408p2/70078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139769557?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330341219" href="/book/show/538843.The_Crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=open_range">open_range</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=i_am_in_love">i_am_in_love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139769557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14850438353047455161">I'll admit it freely- I was unprepared for Cormac McCarthy. Sure, I've heard all the reviews: that he's bleak, despairing, has a dark and twisted worldview, offers little hope for the future, et cetera ad nauseum. It's one thing to hear about this and to know that cracking a Cormac McCarthy book is not going to be an exercise in gumdrops and rainbows, it's a whole other thing to actually open a book and expose yourself to over 400 pages of brutally hard-living and events that shake your faith in</span>
  <span id="freeText14850438353047455161" style="display:none">I'll admit it freely- I was unprepared for Cormac McCarthy. Sure, I've heard all the reviews: that he's bleak, despairing, has a dark and twisted worldview, offers little hope for the future, et cetera ad nauseum. It's one thing to hear about this and to know that cracking a Cormac McCarthy book is not going to be an exercise in gumdrops and rainbows, it's a whole other thing to actually open a book and expose yourself to over 400 pages of brutally hard-living and events that shake your faith in humankind even as you marvel at the tenacity of the human spirit.<br /><br />The second in McCarthy's Border Trilogy, you know within the opening pages that this is a far different beast of a book than the award-winning <i>All The Pretty Horses</i>. The protagonists are similar, both teen boys looking for adventure and meaning on the other side of the thin imaginary line that divides American soil from Mexican. Yet where John Grady Cole descended into Mexico to care for horses and search out love, <i>The Crossing</i>'s Billy Parham is on a quest that makes Sisyphus' efforts look like a lark. After trapping a pregnant wolf that has been preying on his family's cattle, Billy finds he can't bring himself to kill her and instead sets forth to the mountains of Mexico to release her back into the wild. <br /><br />Of course nothing goes as it should and by the time Billy meanders back across the border to the ruins of his life he is hardly the immature youth who first set out on the long road through unfriendly lands. Through a series of events that are better left unwritten here, Billy finds himself drawn to Mexico again and again as the years go by, each journey removing more and more of his ties to the world and rendering him a living ghost haunted by the choices he's made and the vicissitudes of fate that have left him bereft.<br /><br />Much has been made of the brutality and violence in McCarthy's works and I would be remiss if I didn't mention something about them. Don't get me wrong, this book is violent. There are descriptions of extreme brutality that makes you wonder as to the mental stability of the author- a particularly gruesome description of a man's eyes being sucked from his skull by a perverse German still haunt me. It's not only human-on-human violence within these pages, though. Some of the most inexplicable and haunting acts are performed on animals- the wolf, a dog, Billy's horse- which does far more to bring home the nature of the harsh world that Billy exists in.<br /><br />The interesting thing, for me, is that most of the violence happens off-stage and is made known to the reader only through the effects it has on his characters. You don't get the action, but the reaction. It was this remove from the actual violence that made it seem that much more hard-hitting. Even the violence that occurs on-stage is briefly described in favor of longer passages dealing with the characters recovery from these events. It is this, the focus on the consequences of violence rather than the violence itself, that sticks with the reader long after the last page has been turned. <br /><br />McCarthy is a man who has ruminated at great lengths on the darker nature of humanity and it is laid out fantastically herein. Questions of faith in a god that lets such atrocities as described above occur, moments of extreme kindness and charity by the most dispossessed inhabitants of the Sonoran desert, the sheer random nature of some of the events- McCarthy has crafted a fable that cuts right to the heart of humanity and leaves you questioning whether original sin may actually be an affliction which we suffer. At the very least, he's earned a dedicated fan and I can not wait to read the concluding book of the trilogy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14850438353047455161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2085099250" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2085099250" />
    <a title="Evan Leach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach"><img alt="Evan Leach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1384621842p2/7713825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2085099250?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan Leach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan Leach" href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach">Evan Leach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=1990-1999">1990-1999</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=western">western</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2085099250" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8248330370485681341">Like McCarthy’s other work, this second entry in the Border Trilogy (a series of novels set around the border of the U.S. and Mexico near the middle of the 20th century) features lyrical prose, vivid descriptions of landscapes and nature, memorable dialogue and scenes that are hard to forget. This book has even more of a philosophical bent than some of McCarthy’s other novels, as many of the people the protagonist meets on his travels (priests, ex-soldiers, gypsies, etc.) engage him in deep meta</span>
  <span id="freeText8248330370485681341" style="display:none">Like McCarthy’s other work, this second entry in the Border Trilogy (a series of novels set around the border of the U.S. and Mexico near the middle of the 20th century) features lyrical prose, vivid descriptions of landscapes and nature, memorable dialogue and scenes that are hard to forget. This book has even more of a philosophical bent than some of McCarthy’s other novels, as many of the people the protagonist meets on his travels (priests, ex-soldiers, gypsies, etc.) engage him in deep metaphysical conversation during their brief encounters. There are a number of interesting ideas being explored both on and just off the page, such as the contrast between an America gearing up for WWII and a Mexico that seems to be still struggling to enter the 20th century. <br /><br />It’s hard not to compare this to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/469571.All_the_Pretty_Horses__The_Border_Trilogy___1_" title="All the Pretty Horses (The Border Trilogy, #1) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">All the Pretty Horses</a>. While these are somewhat different books (<i>Horses</i> more romantic and unified; <i>Crossing</i> more philosophical and fragmented), I did think <i>Horses</i> was the better novel. One special note: a significant portion of this book’s dialogue – far more than in <i>Horses</i> – is in Spanish. If I was fluent in Spanish, I could see this being a unique touch that gave the story real flavor and authenticity, and possibly a major point in the book’s favor. Unfortunately, my high school Spanish was woefully inadequate, and there is enough Spanish dialogue here that a non-Spanish reader (like myself) may glaze over dialogue critical to revealing characters' motivations or even elements of the plot. This is a book I would love to re-read later in life if I ever gain a better grasp of Spanish, and I would not be surprised if this book is elevated to a 5-star level as a result. Until then, however, I can only speculate on what I might have missed.<br /><br />That said, I enjoyed this book, and I look forward to finishing the trilogy soon. Not quite as magical as the first entry in the series, but a still a strong and fascinating Western. <b>4.5 stars</b>, highly recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8248330370485681341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FsU6BpQ4srC3qsM65z78rrZnmr1uZuebCf59Frja/zfDLHbt5Asd+vrkAlUgf47OWpqwBMidm4jw8QttY8fY9Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wXx65GIDg2uotKRGwRBixteZePZfFXacPmLK3cHI1MIUlTYPEjAsIeX6ZSkGURCmO2RST/nuCo/HbbymGtXzAA==')})
  }

Written in what DeLillo calls &quot;super-omniscience&quot; the sentences sweep from young Cotter Martin as he jumps the gate to the press box, soars over the radio waves, runs out to the diamond, slides in on a fast ball, pops into the stands where J. Edgar Hoover is sitting with a drunken Jackie Gleason and a splenetic Frank Sinatra, and learns of the Soviet Union\'s second detonation of a nuclear bomb. It\'s an absolutely thrilling literary moment. When Bobby Thomson hits Branca\'s pitch into the outstretched hand of Cotter—the &quot;shot heard around the world&quot;—and Jackie Gleason pukes on Sinatra\'s shoes, the events of the next few decades are set in motion, all threaded together by the baseball as it passes from hand to hand.\n\n&quot;It\'s all falling indelibly into the past,&quot; writes DeLillo, a past that he carefully recalls and reconstructs with acute grace. Jump from Giants Stadium to the Nevada desert in 1992, where Nick Shay, who now owns the baseball, reunites with the artist Kara Sax. They had been brief and unlikely lovers 40 years before, and it is largely through the events, spinoffs, and coincidental encounters of their pasts that DeLillo filters the Cold War experience. He believes that &quot;global events may alter how we live in the smallest ways,&quot; and as the book steps back in time to 1951, over the following 800-odd pages, we see just how those events alter lives. This reverse narrative allows the author to strip away the detritus of history and pop culture until we get to the story\'s pure elements: the bomb, the baseball, and the Bronx. In an epilogue as breathless and stunning as the prologue, DeLillo fast-forwards to a near future in which ruthless capitalism, the Internet, and a new, hushed faith have replaced the Cold War\'s blend of dread and euphoria.\n\nThrough fragments and interlaced stories—including those of highway killers, artists, celebrities, conspiracists, gangsters, nuns, and sundry others—DeLillo creates a fragile web of connected experience, a communal Zeitgeist that encompasses the messy whole of five decades of American life, wonderfully distilled.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13342618390822047893\" Before long Andrews strikes up a friendship with one of them, a man who regales Andrews with tales of immense herds of buffalo, ready for the taking, hidden away in a beautiful valley deep in the Colorado Rockies. He convinces Andrews to join in an expedition to track the animals down. The journey out is grueling, but at the end is a place of paradisal richness. Once there, however, the three men abandon themselves to an orgy of slaughter, so caught up in killing buffalo that they lose all sense of time. Winter soon overtakes them: they are snowed in. As they journey by rough stages up the Missouri, they meet with all the dangers, difficulties, beauties, and temptations of the untamed West. For Tasmin, these temptations include Jim Snow, a frontiersman, ferocious Indian fighter, and part-time preacher known up and down the Missouri as &quot;the Sin Killer.&quot;\n  \n    The Wandering Hill\n  \nAbandoning their luxurious steamer, which is stuck in the ice near the Knife River, the Berrybenders make their way overland to the confluence of the Missouri and the Yellowstone rivers to spend the winter in conditions of siege at the trading post of Pierre Boisdeffre. By now, Tasmin is a married woman, or as good as, living with the elusive young mountain man Jim Snow, pregnant with his child and about to discover that he has secrets he hasn\'t told her. For his part, Jim is about to discover that in taking the outspoken, tough-minded, stubbornly practical young aristocratic woman into his teepee he has bitten off more than he can chew... \n  \n    By Sorrow\'s River\n  \nThe Berrybender party once again takes to the trail, across the endless Great Plains of the West towards Santa Fe, where they intend—those who are lucky enough to survive the journey—to spend the winter. Along the way, they meet up with a varied cast of characters from the history of the West—Kit Carson, the famous scout; Le Partezon, the fearsome Sioux war chief; two aristocratic Frenchmen whose eccentric aim is to cross the Great Plains by hot air balloon; a band of raiding Pawnee; and many other astonishing characters who prove once again that the rolling, grassy plains are not, in fact, nearly as empty of life as they look. \n  \n    Folly and Glory\n  \nUnder irksome, though comfortable, arrest with her family in Mexican Santa Fe, Tasmin Berrybender—who would once have followed Jim Snow anywhere—is no longer even sure she likes him, or knows where to go to next. Neither does anyone else—even Captain Clark, of Lewis &amp; Clark fame, is puzzled by the great changes sweeping over the West, replacing red men and buffalo with towns and farms. As the Berrybenders embark on a desperate journey to New Orleans—starving, dying of thirst, and in constant, bloody battle, with slavers pursuing them—both Jim Snow and Tasmin find themselves forced       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy">About Cormac McCarthy</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1593872299p3/4178.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy">Cormac McCarthy
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
17,168
followers

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6846618319401480993">Cormac McCarthy is an American novelist and playwright. He has written ten novels in the Southern Gothic, western, and post-apocalyptic genres and has also written plays and screenplays. He received the Pulitzer Prize in 2007 for <i>The Road</i>, and his 2005 novel <i>No Country for Old Men</i> was adapted as a 2007 film of the same name, which won four Academy Awards, including Best Picture.<br/><br/>His earlier <i>Blood M</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6846618319401480993" style="display:none">Cormac McCarthy is an American novelist and playwright. He has written ten novels in the Southern Gothic, western, and post-apocalyptic genres and has also written plays and screenplays. He received the Pulitzer Prize in 2007 for <i>The Road</i>, and his 2005 novel <i>No Country for Old Men</i> was adapted as a 2007 film of the same name, which won four Academy Awards, including Best Picture.<br /><br />His earlier <i>Blood Meridian</i> (1985) was among <i>Time Magazine's</i> poll of 100 best English-language books published between 1925 and 2005 and he placed joint runner-up for a similar title in a poll taken in 2006 by <i>The New York Times</i> of the best American fiction published in the last 25 years. Literary critic Harold Bloom named him as one of the four major American novelists of his time, along with Thomas Pynchon, Don DeLillo, and Philip Roth. He is frequently compared by modern reviewers to William Faulkner.<br /><br />In 2009, Cormac McCarthy won the PEN/Saul Bellow Award, a lifetime achievement award given by the PEN American Center.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6846618319401480993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
